    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1408791541" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1408791541" />
    <a title="Veronica Ika " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18173496-veronica-ika"><img alt="Veronica Ika " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566828461p2/18173496.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1408791541?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Veronica Ika " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Veronica Ika " href="/user/show/18173496-veronica-ika">Veronica Ika </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18173496-veronica-ika?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18173496-veronica-ika?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18173496-veronica-ika?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18173496-veronica-ika?shelf=three-stars">three-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18173496-veronica-ika?shelf=high-fantasy">high-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1408791541" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7612223329273450093">Some people say that a good book title should include keywords that describe the most important thing, character, or idea in the whole story. And tell me if ‘Apprentice’ here is not one of such keywords! If you have been familiar with the Black Magician trilogy, this word definitely gives you a good idea of what this prequel will be and even the gap between the two works. So, does it mean that those who haven’t read the trilogy cannot enjoy this book? Nah. That depends, though. Those who have re</span>
  <span id="freeText7612223329273450093" style="display:none">Some people say that a good book title should include keywords that describe the most important thing, character, or idea in the whole story. And tell me if ‘Apprentice’ here is not one of such keywords! If you have been familiar with the Black Magician trilogy, this word definitely gives you a good idea of what this prequel will be and even the gap between the two works. So, does it mean that those who haven’t read the trilogy cannot enjoy this book? Nah. That depends, though. Those who have read the trilogy may be curious about some events that have been mentioned many times in the trilogy but not explained in detail because they happened in the past, and so they seek this book to satisfy their curiosity. Those who haven’t read the trilogy may enjoy the large part of the book, but I believe will ponder about the necessity of certain POV that can only be understood by those who have read the trilogy. In this case, I belong to the first group, but to be honest, I wish I read this prequel before the trilogy for several reasons I will elaborate below. <br /><br /><b>
  <u>WHAT THIS PREQUEL IS ACTUALLY ABOUT</u>
</b><br /><br />This story takes place hundred years before Magicians’ Guild exists, when healer and magician are two different entities, during the infamous Sachakan War that leads to the wasteland of Sachaka…<br /><br />Tessia is the daughter of the village healer in the remote village of Mandryn, which is under protection of Lord Dakon, one of few higher magicians of Kyralia. Her mother wants her to marry and start a family, but being an assistant to her father makes her develop an unflinching wish to be a healer. A wish that now is more impossible since her accidentally harnessing magic to defend herself from unwanted advances of Takado, a visiting Sachakan magician. Now Tessia finds herself to be another apprentice of Lord Dakon after Apprentice Jayan, who’s clearly not happy with the arrangement. But Jayan’s resentment and her longing to be a healer are proven trivial because Takado is back to Kyralia with an army of magicians, determined to tear her world apart… Can she, and all of Kyralians, survive the impending war?<br /><br />Stara is a half Elyne and half Sachakan. Returning home to his father, she thought she would help his father with his trade, as she helps her mother with hers in Elyne, where she’s raised. It turns out, to her surprise, that women in Sachaka are no less free than the slaves, that her father wants her back to Sachaka only to be married off to a man of his choice and give birth to an heir to secure his land. Will she just let her father defines her fate? But how can she defy the ingrained social life of Sachakan if it means she will risk the life of her brother’s infertile wife?<br /><br />This book includes two excerpts written by Lord Dannyl -- a particular magician we will know very well from the trilogy -- as the prologue of the book. The first one is the letter to Administrator Lorlen, which I believe is part of his report during his investigation of Akkarin’s past journey, mentioning the Sachakan War. The second one is the extract of the preface of his book entitled A Complete History of Magic, mentioning about how twenty years ago they found out about black magic, known as higher magic, practised by Higher Magicians in Kyralia. Not only that these excerpts tell us about what this story will be, it also shows us that <b>this prequel of the Black Magician trilogy is also related to the next trilogy (sequel), the Spy trilogy.</b><br /><br />Similar to the Black Magician trilogy, <b>this prequel is told from five POVs</b>: Lord Dakon, Tessia, Jayan, Hanara, and Stara. Too much POVs? I can’t agree more. Two or three is okay. But four and more, which is the case for almost Trudi Canavan’s books I have read, are not really okay, because it is difficult to be impartial then. There must be some POVs you find more entertaining to follow than the rest and you might get the urge to skip some ‘boring’ POVs, which I guess is not good if you want to understand the whole story. Or not? This book is about 750 pages, with five parts and fifty chapters. You may think that because the characters only have this book to tell us their story, they will do it in much faster pace. Nope. I think I must accept the fact that <b>Trudi Canavan writes stories in slow pace.</b> While it is necessary in some parts, in others it is infuriating. Those who haven’t read the trilogy will think that Hanara’s and Stara’s POVs are not essential and I can’t blame them. The whole story in this prequel won’t be incomplete without their POVs. I have just read the first book of Spy trilogy before deciding to read this one, and I believe that Hanara’s and Stara’s POVs will make sense if you relate them with the Spy trilogy.<br /><br /><b>After slow-paced four parts, the last part is, shockingly, too fast-paced.</b> After the hard-won battle against the Sachakan that takes almost three parts of the book, the Kyralian decides to become the aggressor instead, and only in more or less five pages manages to take over Sachaka from the spineless Emperor. There is a gap of ten years between the last chapter and the epilogue, so the epilogue is basically the summary of what happened in the past ten years following the invasion to Sachaka. In less than 8 pages we learn that a character becomes a Crazy Emperor, a main character is killed for political reason, and the wasteland of Sachaka is created in less than thirty seconds. I have the right to be angry, right?!?!?! <br /><br />I can’t help from, once again, to condemn the lack of action in this story. Well, I understand that magic at this time of the story (before the Guild) is not as flourished as it is after the Guild. And that’s why I wrote that I wish I read this story first before the trilogy, because I can’t help finding <b>magic in this book so lame and anticlimactic.</b> The higher magicians, under the pressure of the war, gradually learn new strategies in using magic against the Sachakan, all of which, unfortunately, are secret no more to us who have read the trilogy. Even the knowledge of black magic, or the higher magic in this period, is old news, which is disappointing because it is supposed to thrive in this period and isn’t it fair for me to expect that the magicians before the guild will reveal something new about it? But nooo. You can imagine how boring it is for me to re-learn about them! I miss all the action in the trilogy: outwitting the bullying novices, winning the duel, fighting the Ichanis, and so on. The battle of magic in this infamous war is more like this: two opposing armies facing each other in an organized way like chess pieces and throwing strikes at each other, and I can’t stop wondering what they look like during the process. Standing rigid with their hands on their sides or on the shoulders of their fellow magicians? Or crossing their arms across their chests like we do when we pray? I mean, it’s so comical!<br /><br />Learning from The High Lord, I decide <b>not to expect too much from the romance and the ending of this prequel.</b> Oh, don’t worry. There’s a romance in this story, with a happy ending, too, but you won’t find it until practically the last part of the book. Say what?!?!? (I’m not sorry for the spoiler). Well, I can’t ask for more, because it’s a perfect slow burn, and I don’t want them to fall in love too fast, particularly when they tend to dislike each other at first. And speaking about the ending, well, it’s definitely a happy ending, but a happy ending in Trudi Canavan’s universe always has the catch, meaning you will encounter many deaths in this book, too. So prepare yourself!<br /><br /><b>
  <u>MEET THE NARRATORS OF THE STORY</u>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>Tessia, the first Healer Magician/ Magician Healer</i>
</b><br /><br />This character is a disappointment to me, because she’s literally the mirror image of Sonea, only living in different time and setting. She comes from a humble background in a remote village. She is a natural. She is devoted to Healing. And she is a fast learner, too. So forgive me if I constantly imagine Sonea whenever read her POV. She lost both her parents during the war and accepted the misfortune toughly. She even becomes more resolute in her determination to apply magic in healing and achieves it along her way accompanying her magician to war. And so, she is the first magician who’s using magic to heal.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Jayan, the first founder of the Magicians’ Guild</i>
</b><br /><br />The first Apprentice of Lord Dakon, a young man of noble birth, though his relationship with his father is not quite good. At first, he dismisses Tessia for her humble background and devotion to healing, thinking that the girl is ungrateful and wasting Lord Dakon’s time teaching her magic, and thus prolonging his apprenticeship. During the war, he frequently helps her tending to sick and wounded people, and the animosity slowly turns into admiration, to the point that he sees her as an attractive opposite sex. He is the first apprentice to propose to build a guild for magician and seems to fulfil his wish not long after they successfully invade Sachaka. <br /><br /><b>
  <i>Lord Dakon</i>
</b><br /><br />His character, unsurprisingly, reminds me a lot of Lord Rothen, because of his age, his empathy and kindness towards small people, his tendency to choose to be a good man, and his being a guardian to the main heroine. He teaches and cares for his apprentices fairly. He shows a deep remorse for killing slaves to gain power. He’s not a coward but he lacks the backbone to confront his best friend from getting too obsessed with power. He rather puts a distance between himself and the said friend. <br /><br /><b>
  <i>Hanara, the betrayer’s slave</i>
</b><br /><br />One of the POV I actually hesitate to read. Some might say that it is unnecessary to give him the role of a narrator, but I think his POV is actually important in some ways. I even think he is rather important to show us the true character of all slaves of Sachaka. He has beaten to near death by his master for something so trivial, and he fears his master because of that. He’s then be freed and become a free man, serving Lord Dakon. But even with his freedom he feels a deep feeling of unease. He has bred and lived as a slave for all of his life that freedom becomes no salvation for him but rather a loss of purpose. It’s not that he is a heartless or ungrateful man. He feels kindness toward Tessia for saving his life and even Lord Dakon for giving him a job, but again, he, like other slaves of Sachaka, only serves and remains loyal to his master. Despite his fear, he also somewhat feels proud to serve Takado and wants to serve him as best as he can! This is the kind of attitude that will make me label him as stupid beyond comprehension if only the other slaves do not share the same attitude! This kind of loyalty is actually disgusting but at the same time confusing that I still can’t find the logical explanation for. <br /><br />Without his POV, we won’t be able to learn anything about the villain of the story: Takado. Thank goodness that Trudi Canavan has given as a character worthy to be called villain. He gives an impression of a cruel Sachakan Ashaki, beating Hanara near to death and even attempting to defile a girl in his host’s house, but we can’t actually judge him for that, because such behaviour is shockingly very common in his homeland. During the war, Hanara keeps telling us his observation of Takado’s intelligence, how he is so calculated and composed in his attempt to take over Kyralia. Compared to other Sachakans joining his army, these traits are somehow admirable for a villain. <br /><br />Back to Hanara. After his master has dubbed the Betrayer and killed, Hanara still considers Takado a hero. What!? Yeah, I can’t understand him. He is given to Lord Narvellan as a servant and again becomes an observer of another villain until he meets his pitiful end. So, is his character unnecessary? I dare not say so.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Stara, the founder of Traitor’s Sanctuary</i>
</b><br /><br />She makes her first appearance in the third part of the book, to my dismay. With the Kyralian busy fighting against the Sachakan, who will care what happen in Sachaka? I cannot suppress a sigh of frustration every time I arrive at her POV. I think I might be more tolerant if she’s actually the founder of the Traitor, which is not, to my disappointment. She’s also another party affected by the Sachakan war and the ill luck entails, but in truth it’s only an insignificant portion of it, compared to other POVs. So I dare say that this book will be more enjoyable without her POV. All of her story, including how she decides to leave Sachaka and finds the hidden valley along with her brethren to start a women-only society, which is the essential part of her story, can actually be told in a short version in any book in the Spy Trilogy!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7612223329273450093" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3576100052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2452704489019339457">2.75 <br/>I may have lost my taste for epic fantasy. Although this is a fairly standard example of the genre (it has magic, an evil antagonist, a plucky young protagonist, a medieval-like setting, and over 500 pages) it failed to interest me much. Maybe it's just TOO typical of the genre. It's all been done before. Nothing about this story stands out. The politics and economics don't make a lot of sense. The magic system is simplistic. The characters aren't especially engaging. There is no real world</span>
  <span id="freeText2452704489019339457" style="display:none">2.75 <br />I may have lost my taste for epic fantasy. Although this is a fairly standard example of the genre (it has magic, an evil antagonist, a plucky young protagonist, a medieval-like setting, and over 500 pages) it failed to interest me much. Maybe it's just TOO typical of the genre. It's all been done before. Nothing about this story stands out. The politics and economics don't make a lot of sense. The magic system is simplistic. The characters aren't especially engaging. There is no real world relevance in the story, and it offers no particular insights other than to point out that slavery is bad. It's readable, but I can't honestly say I liked it. So, not bad, but not quite 3 stars.</span>

<div id="review_98679226" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/98679226" />
    <a title="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/395599-shannon-giraffe-days"><img alt="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420332941p2/395599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/98679226?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" href="/user/show/395599-shannon-giraffe-days">Shannon (Giraffe Days)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780316037877" href="/book/show/7302397-the-magician-s-apprentice">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=australian-women-writers">australian-women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=2010">2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98679226" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5414860323462864879">Set in the same world, but back in time, as Canavan's first trilogy, the Black Magician Trilogy, this is in a way back-story, and in a way, not. I thought it was a standalone novel about what happened between Kyralia and Sachaka, two neighbouring countries, and more importantly, about how "black magic" - or higher magic as it used to be called - came to be banned and the knowledge of it repressed. Well, it <i>is</i> a standalone Fantasy novel, it <i>is</i> about the war between Kyralia and Sachaka, which is l</span>
  <span id="freeText5414860323462864879" style="display:none">Set in the same world, but back in time, as Canavan's first trilogy, the Black Magician Trilogy, this is in a way back-story, and in a way, not. I thought it was a standalone novel about what happened between Kyralia and Sachaka, two neighbouring countries, and more importantly, about how "black magic" - or higher magic as it used to be called - came to be banned and the knowledge of it repressed. Well, it <i>is</i> a standalone Fantasy novel, it <i>is</i> about the war between Kyralia and Sachaka, which is least important to me because I couldn't remember anything about it from the first trilogy, but it <i>doesn't</i> explain why higher magic became anathema.<br /><br />The blurb was also misleading, and that's just not on. It says: <br /><br /><br /><blockquote>Events are brewing that will lead nations into war, rival magicians into conflict - and spark an act of sorcery so brutal that its effects will be felt for centuries.<br /><br />One young apprentice stands in the path of the coming storm."</blockquote><br /><br /><br />Well, some of it's true and some of it's ... not so much false, as implying things that just don't happen. (It reads less misleadingly now that I've finished it, I have to admit. But that doesn't excuse it.) It's better when I stick to my Don't Read the Blurb plan! See, does this blurb not imply to you, especially the last line, that this is a Fantasy about a person - in this case an apprentice to a magician - who will somehow affect the outcome of this magical war? They will "stand in the path" - that implies they are important, yes? And I'm not sure what brutal act of sorcery they mean. Can I do a better job at describing this? I can try.<br /><br />Tessia is a young woman studying healing under her father, a village healer in Mandryn, in the ley of Aylen, ruled by the magician Lord Dakon. Women can't be healers in Kyralia, but Tessia's determined. When she accompanies her father to Lord Dakon's home to tend to the life-threatening injuries of a man who is the slave of a Sachakan magician travelling through the country, she comes under the lecherous eye of the Sachakan magician, Takado. In defending herself against his advances, she discovers that she's a Natural - someone with magic who, if not trained, could cause great destruction through uncontrolled unleashing of their power.<br /><br />Dakon takes Tessia on as his apprentice, even though he already has one: a young man, Jayan, from an old, rich and powerful family in the capital. Dakon is part of a group of magicians concerned about Takado's purpose in travelling the country, and worried about their leys if Sachaka invades. Once part of the Sachakan empire, Kyralia and another neighbouring country, Elyne, were granted independence - now, landless Sachakans are eyeing what was once theirs and are arrogant and confident enough to believe they can take it back.<br /><br />Dakon has the choice job of approaching the king about their concerns. He takes Tessia and Jayan with him to the capital, Imardin. While there, his fears are realised: Takado and some other Sachakan magicians attack his home village of Mandryn, killing almost everyone. This becomes the pattern of the growing Sachakan "army" as they travel towards Imardin: they burn and destroy villages and drain the people to death of their innate magic, making themselves more and more powerful.<br /><br />In Sachaka, Stara has just arrived from Elyne where she lived with her mother, helping in the family business, to live with her father, a Sachakan. She naïvely believes he asked for her because he wants her to work in his trade with him; instead, she finds him aloof, cold and concerned only with marrying her off to the best possible man for his business.<br /><br />The two stories do eventually merge at the end, but not in any way I was expecting, which was refreshing. Since the war is a magical one, there are none of the usual suspects: no soldiers, no physical fighting. The armies are small, but lethal all the same. Sadly, it's also fairly uneventful, and if Tessia is the apprentice named in the title, then she's barely a main character. Again, there's that implication that a magician's apprentice - which one, we're not sure - is pivotal in the coming battle. In the end, it seems like it was more a convenient and conventional title than any indication of the plot.<br /><br />I greatly enjoyed the Black Magician trilogy, though it has a slow start. The second and third books were very good. I only read the first book of Canavan's second trilogy, The Age of Five: it didn't really grab me, plus I moved countries after the first book and the covers here are just too ugly. I was nevertheless excited about this book, especially as it revisited the world of the first trilogy. It was a largely uninspiring read, though. There were some great scenes, and where there was real action it was pretty engrossing. <br /><br />Stara's story was more fun to read, but she gets less air time than - I can't say "Tessia's story" because it's not her story either. Tessia discovers magical healing, and that was interesting, but the main story, of these two armies of magicians moving about the land, one trying to evacuate people and kill Sachakans, the other killing and destroying everyone and everything, becomes very repetitive and monotonous. There are bits throughout that capture the imagination, but overall it's a simplistic story told simply, and anti-climactic to boot. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5414860323462864879" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3480731478" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3480731478" />
    <a title="Ekmef" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12767858-ekmef"><img alt="Ekmef" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599500478p2/12767858.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3480731478?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ekmef" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ekmef" href="/user/show/12767858-ekmef">Ekmef</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3480731478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14531736865997305043">I bought this book years ago while on holiday in Germany. The idea was to improve my German, but instead I only managed to read the first few chapters a couple of times. I was happy to finally get my hands on the English version. It's not bad but it's not amazing either. The story plods on and on. Major societal issues are highlighted and then simply bypassed. I kind of missed the engagement I felt with the characters in The Magician's Guild.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_245278508" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/245278508" />
    <a title="Mieneke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2485666-mieneke"><img alt="Mieneke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435395776p2/2485666.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/245278508?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mieneke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mieneke" href="/user/show/2485666-mieneke">Mieneke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781841495903" href="/book/show/6575656-the-magician-s-apprentice">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2485666-mieneke?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2485666-mieneke?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2485666-mieneke?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer245278508" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5488914889473641369">Written after <i>The Black Magician</i> trilogy, <i>The Magician's Apprentice</i> is a prequel of sorts to said series. As a step back into history, it is a very cool story, showing us events in the past that set things in motion that will not only feature in <i>The Black Magician</i> trilogy, but also in <i>The Traitor Spy</i> trilogy. To my surprise, however, the book dealt with a different era than I expected for some reason. Why, I don't know, but I expected the book to either deal with Akkarin's history, or the period</span>
  <span id="freeText5488914889473641369" style="display:none">Written after <i>The Black Magician</i> trilogy, <i>The Magician's Apprentice</i> is a prequel of sorts to said series. As a step back into history, it is a very cool story, showing us events in the past that set things in motion that will not only feature in <i>The Black Magician</i> trilogy, but also in <i>The Traitor Spy</i> trilogy. To my surprise, however, the book dealt with a different era than I expected for some reason. Why, I don't know, but I expected the book to either deal with Akkarin's history, or the period in time when black magic became forbidden by the Guild. Instead, we get the story of the Sachakan war and why the Allied countries become allied against it. I truly enjoyed the story and while this book is the biggest of the Kyralia books so far, I couldn't put it down and blitzed through it in four days.<br /><br />We get five separate points of view in this book: Tessia, the titular apprentice, Dakon, her master, Jayen, Dakon's other apprentice, Stara, a Sachakan woman, and Hanara, a Source slave for one of the Sachakan High Mages. Canavan uses these PoV's cleverly to shed light on all sides of the conflict. We're not only given insight into both the Kyralian and the Sachakan sides, but we also get different viewpoints within these camps, from different power levels. I found this an interesting approach and while I definitely had some favourite viewpoints, none of them had me reading along impatiently to get past it to a new one.<br /><br />My favourite viewpoints were Tessia and Stara. Tessia is the main character of the book and very likeable. She's a conscientious young woman, with a passion for healing that, due to the prejudices of Kyralian society, she won't be able to pursue professionally, as women Healers aren't accepted by the Guild. Still, she doesn't give up and keeps looking for a way to use her knowledge and skills to Heal, even once she is apprenticed to Dakon as a magician. She shows spirit throughout the novel, but still manages to grow (up) as a character during the narrative. What made Tessia's story even more interesting, was the fact that here we see the genesis of Healing magic. Tessia literally discovers it as we read along and it is very interesting to see how much of it is derived from knowledge of black magic. If the magicians of Sonea's time had known this, they would have shuddered in revulsion!<br /><br />My other favourite point of view and character was Stara. She's our window into Sachakan culture. Being half-Sachakan herself and mainly raised in the liberal country of Elyne, Stara is as alienated by the conservative and repressive Sachakan society as the reader. We're shown how this society could produce rebels such as the Ichani, who are considered without honour, and how the Ichani aren't the only rebels in Sachaka. Stara's story is also a nice set up for <i>The Traitor Spy</i> trilogy, even though I didn't realise this until I started that series. Stara is a strong female character, who doesn't let herself be broken to the Sachakan view of what is proper for a woman. I enjoyed how much her upbringing in Elyne coloured her perception of Sachaka and how she is shown that not all is how it seems, both by her brother and her slave/confidante Vora. Her relationships with Vora and the wives of her husband's friends are key to her story's ending and I found the different dynamics between the women (and their husbands) very interesting. They all have marital problems, but none of them have the same kind of problem. Still they form a tight-knit circle and support each other how and where they can. In Tessia and her female friends and Stara and her circle of friends Canavan creates a strong example of female strength and empowerment, through their sheer determination not to be put in the places society, whether Kyralian or Sachakan, deems fit for them.<br /><br />In addition to a deeper understanding of the history of Kyrelia and its world, in <i>The Magician's Apprentice</i>, Canavan gives us a gripping tale of war and adventure. I really enjoyed this book, both as a prequel to the previous trilogy and as a standalone story. It isn't necessary to have read <i>The Black Magician</i> trilogy before this one, however, there are so many clues for <i>The Traitor Spy</i> trilogy (or at least book one, <i>The Ambassador's Mission</i>) that I'd recommend reading this one before starting that series at least. If you haven't read any Canavan before and you don't want to commit to a full trilogy, <i>The Magician's Apprentice</i> is an excellent way to get yourself acquainted with this author. If you have read her work before – and enjoyed it – then this novel should be a welcome return to the world of Kyrelia and the Magicians' Guild.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5488914889473641369" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

	<tr>
		<td>
		    <b>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fXs5kN+/ZtBvt/icRTlAjhjZ45i2tqeUGYXk08kawBtHXVBCZOGvuMicTP2yzEpRzoYEUHg7KfNC+6JiBFkygQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ikusiMk5E6r/WV88y0VgBqhwZUWFWQdq8KerQ0UcQc+wbcVacmfawlhy6108sGrZfi+CjUvUiQ2r2e3yiF+zVQ==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/15890.Trudi_Canavan">About Trudi Canavan</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4292451654411827152">Trudi Canavan was born in Kew, Melbourne and grew up in Ferntree Gully, a suburb at the foothills of the Dandenongs.<br/><br/>In 1999 she won the Aurealis Award for Best Fantasy Short Story with “Whispers of the Mist Children”. In the same year she was granted a writers residency at Varuna Writers’ Centre in Katoomba, New South Wales.<br/><br/>In November 2001, The Magicians’ Guild was first published in Australia. T</span>
  <span id="freeText4292451654411827152" style="display:none">Trudi Canavan was born in Kew, Melbourne and grew up in Ferntree Gully, a suburb at the foothills of the Dandenongs.<br /><br />In 1999 she won the Aurealis Award for Best Fantasy Short Story with “Whispers of the Mist Children”. In the same year she was granted a writers residency at Varuna Writers’ Centre in Katoomba, New South Wales.<br /><br />In November 2001, The Magicians’ Guild was first published in Australia. The second book of the trilogy, The Novice, was published in June 2002 and was nominated for the Aurealis Award for Best Fantasy Novel. The third book The High Lord was released in January 2003 and was nominated for the Best Novel Ditmar category. All three books entered Australian top ten SF bestseller lists.<br /><br />The Black Magician Trilogy reached the international market in 2004, published by HarperCollins’ EOS imprint in North America and Orbit Books in the UK. The trilogy is now rated by Nielsen BookScan as the most successful debut fantasy series of the last 10 years.<br /><br />Trudi’s second trilogy, Age of the Five, has also enjoyed bestselling success. Priestess of the White reached No.3 in the Sunday Times hardback fiction bestseller list, staying in the top ten for six weeks.<br /><br />In early 2006 Trudi signed a seven-figure contract with Orbit to write the prequel and sequel to the Black Magician Trilogy. The prequel, The Magician’s Apprentice was released in 2009 and won the Best Fantasy Novel category of the Aurealis Awards. She is now working on the sequel trilogy, and planning her next fantasy series, which will be set in an entirely new world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4292451654411827152" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
